Each card carried status of soil with respect of 12 parameters and crop-wise recommendations of soil amendment and fertilizer including N fertilizer to improve productivity through judicious use of inputs. Linking the soil health card initiative with N management tools will further help meet the N challenge. There is a huge opportunity for India to address the low NUE of agriculture and with this impact positively on SDGs 3, 6, 7, 11, 13, 14 and 15. With the support of enabling government policies around 30% of the emission of N2 O can be reduced as against the business-as-usual by 2030 from agriculture sector itself with implementation of current technologies at scale (Sapkota et al., 2019 ). This will translate into emission saving of about 17.5 mt CO2 e year− 1  without compromising the yield. Supportive policies and well-equipped extension systems are however required to implement the large-scale use of these generally knowledge-intensive practices.   8.4 A case of optimal: USA 8.4.1 Regional context Total world N fertilizer consumption in 2013/14 was estimated at 110.4 Tg N, of which the USA consumed 11% (FAI, 2017 ). North America is the world's largest producer and exporter of commercial fertilizers and is third only to China and India as the largest consumer. These countries have an aggregate market share of 57% for all N fertilizer consumption. Changing diets and demand for consistent food supply increased demands on North American farmers to produce more food, which resulted in increasing use of synthetic N fertilizer. Nitrogen use in the USA increased about 10-fold from 1900 to 2000. There was an exponential increase in synthetic N use from 1960 to around 1980s followed by leveling at around 10–12 Tg N year− 1 . A slowdown in the increase of synthetic fertilizer inputs from the 1980s has resulted, although better agronomic practice has improved the efficiency of N use leading to a corresponding increase in yields of the most important crops (Alston et al., 2010 ; Howarth et al., 2002 ). About 40% of the land in the United States is used for agriculture of some form, which includes about 175 million hectares (mha) of croplands and 200 mha of pasture. The largest single crop is maize with a 2016 production of 385 million tons (mt), followed by soybeans with 117 mt, and wheat with 63 mt. Other major crops include sugarcane, sugar beets, potatoes, and bananas. Much of the synthetic N fertilizer is used in areas where maize and wheat are grown (Fig. 14  ). The Midwestern states—Iowa, Illinois, Indiana, Ohio—are the principal areas for cultivation of corn and are frequently referred to as the Corn Belt. Wheat is grown in the broad, open lands of the Great Plains, in Kansas, Nebraska, North and South Dakota, and the Canadian provinces of Alberta and Saskatchewan. Considering yield and economic returns, farmers in the United States are among the most productive in the world because of continuing improvements in genetics and agronomy that, with highly supportive policies, make adoption of improved technologies relatively easy. The United States produces about half of the world's maize and 10% of its wheat (https://www.ers.usda.gov/data-products.aspx ). In North America, agriculture is highly mechanized and heavily dependent upon an integrated system of supporting agribusinesses, extension services, and accommodating governmental policies and safety nets. Although farmers represented less than 2% of the U.S. population, they successfully fed the country at a high standard of living, produced grain and other products for export, and still maintained a surplus carryover of 2% of the total production. Although N fertilizer use in the USA has increased more slowly in the past two decades (Fig. 15  ), yields of all major crops have continued to increase. Because crop yields are closely related to N uptake (Cassman et al., 2002 ), these trends imply a steady increase in NUE and reduced N losses to the environment. Cassman et al. (2002)  suggested that greater NUE has resulted from two factors: (1) steady improvement in the stress tolerance of maize hybrids that increases crop growth rates and allows sowing at higher plant densities, which together accelerate the establishment of a vigorous root system to intercept and acquire available N in the soil profile, and (2) development and adoption of technologies that may improve the synchrony between crop N demand and N supply. In addition to efforts to sustain soil quality, technologies comprising good agronomy include (a) fertilizer N application based on integrated management programs that incorporate soil test-based evidence, (b) split N fertilizer applications, (c) fertigation (the application of nitrogen and other nutrients through irrigation), (d) site-specific N management, and (e) new fertilizer formulations such as controlled release and nitrification inhibitors. Available data suggest that maize receiving the largest share of total N fertilizer in the U.S. (44% in 2005), had remarkably low NUE in the 1960s but resulted in impressive improvements of 50% NUE from the mid-1970s to early 2000s (Fig. 16  ). For example, average NUE in the U.S. required 1.0 kg of applied N to produce 43 kg of grain yield during 1974–76, whereas that same amount of N produced 65 kg of grain during 2003–05 (USEPA, 2011 ). Such improvement in NUE resulted not only from improved crop varieties and good fertilizer management but also due to good policies and larger investment in research and education. Although considerable progress has been made in reducing N surplus and associated environmental problems, significant efforts are still needed to address the secondary N pollution problems (Bricker et al., 2008 ; USEPA, 2009, 2016 ).  8.4.2 From “improved” to “maximal” N use efficiency in USA Cassman et al. (2002)  observed that despite a steady improvement in NUE in maize, current levels of N fertilizer uptake efficiency appear to be relatively low, although data from production-scale studies are few. Despite increasing interest and strong cost-saving incentives, most farmers do not use all the best agronomic and N management practices regarding nitrogen fertilizer management. For example, the U.S. Department of Agriculture Economic Research Service (USDA-ERS) Agricultural Resources and Environmental Indicators (AREI) report indicated that a majority of farmers still apply N in the fall, which leaves N vulnerable to losses during the winter, and gives the lowest fertilizer uptake efficiency and highest NR  losses compared to application in spring or during the summer crop growth period (USDA-ERS, 2006 ). In a recent study, Swaney et al. (2018)  investigated regional crop production and NUE of major U.S. crops over 1987–2012 by considering all the inputs of N (synthetic N, manure, BNF and atmospheric deposition). Significant regional differences in the relationships between N inputs and crop production were observed. Regions with the steepest slopes and highest asymptotes were those dominated by high yielding and biomass producing crops (such as maize, soybeans, alfalfa hay) in Heartland, Northern Crescent and Mississippi Portal. Nevertheless, there was a small but significant overall decrease in NUE (grain yield per unit of N input) on a decadal scale, averaging about 49% in 1987–97 and 45% in 2002–12. This may be because crop production is not increasing as fast as is the rising proportion of synthetic fertilizer N in most regions (Swaney et al., 2018 ). These findings suggest that even in agricultural systems of the United States, where many advances in efficient N use have been made, the need to move toward maximizing NUE remains. The USEPA (2011)  contemplated several potential strategies to maximize NUE: (a) use of controlled release fertilizers, (b) comprehensive updates to nutrient rate and application recommendations, (c) use of real-time, in-season, decision-making tools, (d) development of new, alternative crop production systems with greater integration of legume cover crops, biologically diverse crop rotations, and livestock production.  8.4.3 Institutions and policies influencing fertilizer N consumption In the U.S., the Environmental Protection Agency (EPA) is charged with managing and conducting research on reactive N through the offices of (a) Air and Radiation, (b) Water, and (c) Research and Development. Related to reactive N, there are three important acts in place: Clean Air Act (1970), Clean Water Act (1972), and Safe Drinking Water Act (1974), each of which stipulates thresholds for dangerous levels of NOx and NO3  (USEPA, 2011 ). Other legislations such as the 2007 Energy Independence and Security Act (EISA), and the 1973 Endangered Species Act (ESA) contain provisions that could result in regulatory actions that affect N management on farmlands—particularly those within sensitive watersheds or adjacent to nature reserves or crucial habitat. There are also regional policies such as the Gulf Hypoxia Action Plan to reduce N pollution of the Mexican Gulf marine ecosystem through adoption of agricultural N best management practices (Mississippi River/Gulf of Mexico Watershed Nutrient Task Force, 2008). These acts and policies have been amended as and when required. The EPA develops criteria and sets various standards for emissions/pollutants but has at times been critiqued for not sufficiently addressing these issues. The EPA's Office of Research and Development provides scientific basis for the development of standards, identify and quantify the positive and negative impacts on ecosystem services resulting from the N loadings. Among various other functions, the EPA organizes communication and education and intergovernmental and international cooperation.    9 Desired NUE targets and strategies for achieving the sustainable development goals 9.1 Global NUE targets and projected N demand for 2050 The projections show that feeding a world population of 9.7 billion people in 2050 would require raising overall food production by some 70% between 2010 and 2050 (FAO, 2018 ). The demand for N input would increase to meet the growing future food demand if the efficiencies of N use remains low. Zhang et al. (2015)  proposed NUE (outputs or N yield/sum of all N inputs) targets to increase from 42% (2010) to 67% to meet 2050 global food demand while meeting the SDGs relating to agriculture. If NUE of 67% is achieved, the 2050 global food demand will require 160 Tg input of N year− 1  which is about 8% lower than that of 2010 estimate. This target enforces surplus N (sum of N inputs minus N outputs) to not exceed 50 Tg N ha− 1  year− 1  (decrease of 50% of 2010) assessed for acceptable air and water quality (Bodirsky et al., 2014 ). Their projections show that globally NUE should increase from 42% to 67% whereas the increases in the regions with current excess use of N (i.e., China and India) will be from 25% to 60%. The regions (i.e., U.S./Canada and Europe), which already have high NUE may further increase it to 75%, and the regions with sub-optimal use of N (i.e., sub-Saharan Africa) should continue to maintain the same around 75%. It should be noted that the N input estimates of Zhang et al. (2015)  includes sum of all the sources of N (synthetic, manure, BNF and deposition). Therefore, the estimates of demand of synthetic N alone for 2050 would be much lower. Additionally, it appears that the NUE target of 67% assumes same efficiency of all the sources of N which however may not be the case (Ladha and Chakraborty (2016) ). Another exercise using crop-based approach by Integer and LMC (2013)  projected the global synthetic N demand to increase by 1.3% annually from 101 Tg N in 2010 to 132 Tg N in 2030. Cereals may account for 46% of the increase in world synthetic N demand. The demand for cereals is projected at 3 billion tonnes (Bt) by 2050, up by 43% of today's use, (Alexandratos and Bruinsma, 2012 ) to feed an additional 2.3 billion mouths. The likely change in land area under three globally important cereals (maize, rice and wheat) until 2050 is projected to increase by about 7% from today's total of 542 mha. Using these projections of changes in food demand and harvested land area, we evaluated three NUE/REN  scenarios of global synthetic fertilizer N requirements by maize, rice and wheat to 2050 (Table 5  ). The scenarios we used included current-2010 (average REN  = 0.47), 15% higher REN  than 2010 (average REN  = 0.54) and 15% lower than 2010 (average REN  = 0.40). Since cereal grain demand and harvested area are fixed, N harvested through crop uptake remains the same for all the three scenarios. Compared to 2010, total N harvest by three cereals would increase by 45% with an increase in global cereal production from 2.20 to 3.15 Bt in 2050. To achieve this, an additional 22.6 Tg of N will be required, making it a total of 85.4 Tg of synthetic N application. The average N application would therefore be 166 kg ha− 1  for maize, 151 kg ha− 1  for rice and 129 kg ha− 1  for wheat. It is noteworthy that the release of reactive N into the environment from synthetic N alone in 2050 is estimated at 22% to 114% higher compared to 2010 in the three REN  scenarios. Earlier Cassman et al. (2003)  estimated synthetic N requirement in 2025 by the three major cereals keeping 2000 as the base year. Their projections ranged from 52 to 72 Tg under various scenarios of projected yields, harvested areas and NUE. The NUE scenarios were similar to our analysis, but instead of REN , authors used PFP-N (kg grain per kg of applied fertilizer N). Wood et al. (2004)  reviewed published reports on global scale projections of synthetic N requirement and provided their own two contemporary sets of projections. Their 2050 projections for all the cereals ranged from 112 to 171 Tg synthetic N, which were based on trend analysis and future food needs. A much higher projection of 171 Tg synthetic N assumed constant NUE and the need of soil rehabilitation. Achieving these targets of improving NUE is ambitious but technically feasible. It is also important to remember that there are also biological limits to achieve very high NUE. Nitrogen is highly dynamic and goes through numerous biochemical processes restricting our ability to reduce or eliminate losses completely. Climatic and other agronomic conditions which are sub-optimal to crop production are also responsible for poor NUEs. Due to poor agronomy, many soils have reached to lowest levels of SOM losing abilities to hold and regulate the supply of N to crops when it needs the most. On the other hand, reasons of excess (or misuse) use of N also include poor knowledge and heavy price subsidy. Therefore, a combination of approaches of improved system management, right policies and good campaign/education is needed to improve NUE to meet the SDGs. It should be mentioned here that maize, rice and wheat obtain only 48% of their N from synthetic fertilizer and remaining 52% come from other sources (soil organic N, manure, crop residue, deposition and non-symbiotic BNF) (Ladha et al., 2016a ). Unlike synthetic N, it is hard to project the supply of N from other sources on a global basis. Soil N supply may not change because most cultivated soils have reached a near steady state. Manure N supply may be affected because of labor shortage but residue recycling may increase if CA becomes more popular among farmers (Tallis et al., 2017 ). Inputs from deposition may increase in proportion to increase in synthetic fertilizer N but would also depend on the future improvements in REN . Non-symbiotic BNF, a widespread process largely depends on the availability of C substrate, which may increase with crop productivity and improved management including CA practices.  9.2 Synchronizing crop N demand with N supply Our goal should be to synchronize N supply with crop demand in order to maximize the NUE and minimize N losses. Synchronization implies that the rate at which N becomes available to the plant is closely related to the rate at which the growing crop needs it. High crop N demand linked to maximal genetic yield potential and harvest index of a crop will ensure high synchrony and thereby high NUE, which can be achieved through sound agronomic management practices including the deployment of biodiversity in the form of cover crops, and with genetic crop improvements. From the N supply side, it is essential to adopt more efficient sources of fertilizer, agronomic practices to synchronize N supply with demand, and soil, water and crop management (e.g., CA practices) to ensure a better and favorable production condition. Holistic management approaches will maximize crop N uptake, minimize N losses and optimize indigenous soil N supply including non-symbiotic N fixation by maintaining soil biodiversity and health. Although crop N recovery efficiencies of less than 50% are most common, efficiency levels as high as 70% to 90% have been obtained in well-managed irrigated cereal systems where fertilizer N is precisely supplied to meet crop demand (Chen et al., 2014 ; Grassini and Cassman, 2012 ; Ladha et al., 2005 ; Peng and Cassman, 1998 ; Pittelkow et al., 2017 ). Nutrients are used most efficiently when their availability is synchronized with crop demand. There are many management approaches available for increasing NUE through matching N supply with crop demand and thereby mitigating loss of N from agroecosystems. Some of the approaches have relatively higher relevance and impact in some regions and to some of SDGs (Table 6  ). Managing the right source at the right rate, right time and in the right place is best accomplished with the right tools and best management practices (see Section 8.3.2 ). A range of precision N tools (e.g., Green Seeker™ and the Nutrient Expert® decision support software) and techniques (e.g., direct drilling of fertilizer and fertigation) are now available to support best fertilizer management (Sapkota et al., 2014 ). These tools and techniques provide a means of fine-tuning N management decisions. For example, chlorophyll meter, the leaf color chart or optical sensors (e.g., the GreenSeeker that provides normalized differential vegetation index measurements) can be used to guide in-season N management (Sapkota et al., 2014 ). Similarly, computer/android phone-based decision support software (e.g., Nutrient Expert, Crop Manager) are being used to refine N management practices in farmers' fields in India, Mexico, and the United States. Such tools have become increasingly important in geographies where blanket fertilizer recommendations are the norm. Research by CIMMYT demonstrates that in a rice-wheat system, fertilizer efficiency can be increased by 10–15% due to better placement of fertilizer mechanized seed and fertilizer drilling compared with broadcasting in the conventional system (M.L. Jat, Personal Communication). In the Indo-Gangetic plains of India, both the Nutrient Expert® and GreenSeeker™-based nutrient management have increased the partial factor productivity of nitrogen in wheat compared with state-recommended and farmers' fertilizer practice (Sapkota et al., 2014 ) (Fig. 17  ). Through on-farm comparison in over 4000 farmers' field across Indo-Gangetic plains of India, CIMMYT found that the “nutrient expert”-based nutrient management reduced the GHG intensity of rice, wheat and maize production by 5–35% (average 13%). Similarly, Bijay-Singh (2016)  showed significant improvement in rice yield, agronomic efficiency and recovery efficiency of N through GreenSeeker™ optical sensor based N application in rice in Punjab, India. Likewise, the International Rice Research Institute in collaboration with national partners across Asia developed an internet-based decision support tool named “Nutrient Manager for Rice” (Buresh et al., 2014 ; Sharma et al., 2019 ), which uses the principles of site-specific nutrient management (SSNM), refined through years of research (Buresh et al., 2010 ; Dobermann et al., 2004 ), to calculate field-specific fertilizer N, P, and K recommendations. As discussed earlier, an important cause for low NUE is the application of excess N but equally important is the application of insufficient N causing an optimal crop production. Undoubtedly there are opportunities but also challenges to estimating optimal N rates in environments with huge spatial and temporal variability. Therefore, SSNM based Nutrient Manger which is now upgraded to “Rice Crop Manager” (RCM) (http://cropmanager.irri.org ) enables extension professionals to provide the farmers with personalized field-specific crop and nutrient management recommendations designed to help in increasing yield, nutrient use efficiency, and net economic returns. The RCM calculates fertilizer N rates based on a target yield (Buresh, 2015 ), and distributes its application through several doses matching with the critical growth stages of the crop (Peng and Cassman, 1998 ; Witt et al., 2007 ). The RCM has been released and used by national programs in several Asian countries (http://cropmanager.irri.org ). Its use is greatest in the Philippines where it has been shown in researcher-managed trials to increase yield and net income for rice farmers (Banayo et al., 2018 ). National programs in the Philippines used RCM to provide more than 1.8 million personalized field-specific recommendations, including for N fertilizer, to rice farmers between its release in November 2013 and April 2019 (https://phapps.irri.org/ph/rasstat ). The RCM in the Philippines has since 2016 been part of an advisory service (https://phapps.irri.org/ph/rcm ), which enables staff within the national rice program to collect, monitor, and use information on farmer's practice to further improve and target information for rice farmers, including field and farmer specific N fertilizer management. This includes the automatic delivery to farmers' phones of text (SMS) messages informing farmers of the date to apply a specific quantity of fertilizer to each of their rice fields with a predetermined size, planting date, and variety. Soil, through its biological, chemical and physical characteristics plays an essential role in sustaining and regulating N supply. Hence a “healthy” soil with optimal quantity and quality of organic carbon is crucial for achieving a greater synchrony between crop demand and supply. Irrespective of source, N first enters the soil N pool and is released in mineral form after which it becomes available to the crop. Unlike synthetic N, plant residues or manure undergo a microbially mediated mineralization process, resulting in the slow release of nitrogen that, unlike the pulses of N available with synthetic fertilizer, will slowly supply the crop over the season (Becker et al., 1994 ).  9.3 Ecosystem-based approaches to improving NUE Drinkwater and Snapp (2007)  argued that many N management techniques aiming to resolve the temporal lack of synchronicity between synthetic N application and crop retrieval were developed before the ecosystem concept was widely understood in the context of agricultural systems. As a result, most efforts to improve NUE have focussed on timing (application to match crop phenology and nutrient demand), and on the placement of fertilizer within the root zone (e.g., through fertigation, knifing, banding, deep placement, etc.), rather than through methods aimed at increasing ecosystem functioning to sustain C and N reservoirs that facilitate N mineralization and supply over time. Key to this approach is the development of cropping systems inclusive of cover crops and improved fallows that approach a balanced steady state, in which N exports through harvests are matched by resident soil N reservoirs of approximately equal proportions. Within this context, Drinkwater and Snapp (2007)  also argued that in addition to increasing yield, synthetic fertilizer N should be thought of as a management tool to increase plant uptake, decomposition and C supply, and as a supplementary energy source for heterotrophs that assimilate of N into soil storage reservoirs. Over time, these reservoirs provide slow but potentially consistent release of N to the crop. Synthetic N can also be used to prime soil microbial communities to more rapidly mineralize recalcitrant pools allowing slow but steady release N to the crop. Viable methods to couple agronomic management with mechanisms to increase desirable microbial diversity to mediate N transformations, including decomposition and soil N supply, biologically mediated weathering, and soil aggregation processes, are under researched but nonetheless crucial (Benckiser et al., 2016 ). The restoration of ecosystem functioning by deliberately encouraging crop-associated N-fixing organisms and N-mycorrhizal interactions through microbiome manipulation can also be valuable (Ladha and Reddy, 2019 ). Marschner and Dell (1994)  for example showed that on degraded soils, increasing interactions with mycorrhizae can increase crop uptake of NO3 −  and NH4 + , thereby contributing to improved N supply to the crop. To quantitatively understand that the transformation processes of residues and/or manure-derived N and the factors controlling their entry into different pools within agroecosystems, it is important to develop robust estimates of soil N and C reservoirs under a diversity of cropping systems and soil types. Transformation of organic matter into plant-available N is partially dependent on the lignin to N ratio (L/N), which determines the pattern of N release from organic material (Becker et al., 1994 ; Palm and Sanchez, 1991 ; Singh et al., 2001 ). Deliberate selection of cover or accessory crops species that can contribute to soil ecosystem functioning and N regulation therefore becomes an important consideration in the application of ecosystem-based approaches to improving the crop N economy. For example, in place of bare fallows, cover crops can be chosen that have (a) low L/N ratios, (b) high rates of leaf litter, (c) high rates of root exudation and root mass turnover, and (d) N fixation characteristics. Each of these characteristics will influence soil C and N cycling leading to slow release of N (Connor et al., 2011 ). Soils that have consistent cover crops or application of manure are more likely to be richer in SOM. This in turn increases their ability to retain fertilizer N not used by heterotrophs or taken up by the crop, thereby limiting the losses and pollution. Since crops and particularly cereals receive a significant of their N requirement from non-symbiotic BNF (Ladha et al., 2016a ), creating a conducive soil environment for BNF is also important to improve NUE. Our goal should be to ensure soil conditions which promote biological inputs of N and overall efficient use of all the sources of N (Kundu and Ladha, 1995 ). As global yields have increased, the production of crop residue has also increased. Residues are burnt in many parts of world and can be a valuable organic resource to contribute to soil C and N reservoirs, if managed wisely (Singh and Sidhu, 2014 ; Tallis et al., 2017 ). Farmers making use of the CA can avoid tillage and leave crop residue on the soil surface. This approach can help improve soil quality and influences the regulation of C and N supply to match crop demand (Sapkota et al., 2014, 2017 ). In flooded soils, mixing of cereal crop residues of high L/N with that of legume residue of low L/N has shown to improve synchrony between N supply and rice N demand. This can result in lower N losses and a larger soil N reservoir that can benefit the succeeding rice crop (Becker et al., 1994 ). Bouldin (1988)  inferred that the “fast N fraction” determines the potential supply of N to the current crop. The remaining fraction decomposes more slowly and influences residual and multi-crop effects. Consequently, increasing the “slow N fraction” may have long-term residual effects on N supply and the potential productivity of crop soils. The crucial role of crop residues, manures, and soils rich in decomposer organisms in influencing N mineralization and optimizing the synchrony of N supply and crop N demand deserves further attention to improve NUE. Maintaining and managing crop residues in agriculture can be economically beneficial to many farmers and more importantly to the society (Tallis et al., 2017 ).  9.4 Genetic improvement to increase NUE Genetic improvement of NUE through plant selection, breeding and genetic engineering has also been considered (Bogard et al., 2010 ; Ciampitti and Vyn, 2012 ; Haegele et al., 2013 ; Han et al., 2015 ; Hirel et al., 2007 ; Ladha et al., 1998 ; Lafitte and Edmeades, 1995 ; Lammerts van Bueren and Struik, 2017 ; Martin et al., 2006 ; McAllister et al., 2012 ; Sylvester-Bradley and Kindred, 2009 ). However, to our knowledge, there has been no breeding effort per se  which aimed directly at improving fertilizer recovery efficiency. Instead studies focussed characterizing NUE related traits in diverse crop germplasm differing in grain yield potential. Broadly, two plant traits related to NUE are involved: N acquisition from soil and fertilizer N, and internal efficiency with which N is used to produce plant biomass and grains (Fig. 18  ). Differences in the efficiency of N acquisition may arise from differences in the (a) efficiency of absorption and assimilation of NH4 +  and other N species; (b) root-induced changes in the rhizosphere affecting N mineralization, transformation and transport; and (c) root- and rhizosphere-associated BNF. Variations in the efficiency of internal use may arise from variances in (a) internal N requirements for growth, biomass production, and organ formation, (b) ability to translocate, distribute and remobilize absorbed N in various organs, (c) flag leaf N import/export and leaf senescence patterns, and (d) the efficiency of N use in converting CO2  to carbohydrates. The N acquisition trait of NUE appears to be important when N supply is limiting, which may exist in an unfavorable environment (Anzoua et al., 2010 ; Lafitte and Edmeades, 1995 ; Moll et al., 1982 ; Ortiz-Monasterio et al., 1997 ; Rao et al., 2018 ). However, both N acquisition and internal N efficiency traits in a favorable environment with non-limiting N supply appear to have already been taken care of in the high yielding varieties with high harvest indices (Peng and Cassman, 1998 ). Crop varieties with similar harvest indices may have slightly improved NUE through lower N concentrations in grain and straw, which however may not be acceptable to the consumers because this would reduce the protein contents in the produce (Ladha et al., 1998 ; Singh et al., 1998 ; Sylvester-Bradley and Kindred, 2009 ). In maize, more recent varieties have substantial improvement in internal N efficiency resulting in a lower grain N concentration (Ciampitti and Vyn, 2012 ; Scott et al., 2006 ; Singh et al., 1998 ), which again suggests that the improvement in NUE may have resulted from overall breeding for high grain yields. The selection and breeding efforts to improve crop NUE is also challenged from the fact that there are huge genotype environment and management (G × E × M) interactions. From a comprehensive review, Ciampitti and Vyn (2012)  concluded that the future improvements in grain yield can increase the N recovery efficiency as concomitant effect of the strong linkages between grain, harvest index and N harvest index, and consequently promote further gains in total plant N uptake. The authors further suggest that higher NUE will need combined efforts into (a) determination of appropriate N rates for the specific G × E × M interaction environments, (b) improving plant N uptake (related to processes at soil- and plant-levels), and (c) augmenting the sink demand with sustainable source (i.e., functional photosynthesis-stay green). However, it is noteworthy that in case of rice, Peng and Cassman (1998)  showed that N uptake capacity is not a constraint in modern cultivars with high yield potential provided an optimal N supply exists at times of high crop N demand. Improving the NUE of crop through gene manipulation has also been proposed. Numerous genes involved in N uptake, translocation, and remobilization; amino acid biosynthesis; C and N storage and metabolism; signaling targets; and regulatory elements are being explored (McAllister et al., 2012 ). Some of the genes linked to ammonium assimilation are believed to improve NUE even though they are yet to show any positive effect in engineered plants. On the other hand, over expressing alanine aminotransferase (AlaAT), a gene not related to N metabolism has resulted in improved biomass, yield and N content in rice grown under N-limiting condition (Shrawat et al., 2008 ). It will be interesting to investigate if AlaAT or other genes hypothesized to affect NUE would result in additional gains in improved crop genotypes which breeders have already developed for high yield potential, harvest index and REN  (Fischer and Edmeades, 2010 ).  9.5 Engineering cereals to fix their own N There is an inverse relationship between N loss and N synchrony. An almost absolute synchrony between N supply and N demand would result in no or negligible loss of N, similar to N2 -fixing legume-rhizobium symbiosis where demand and supply are tightly linked. Absolute synchrony like that of a legume will not be attainable in fertilizer-dependent cereal production systems unless symbiotic N2  fixation ability is assembled in the cereals. Endowing crops, particularly cereals with the capacity to produce their own N has been an ambitious goal of plant biologists (Beatty and Good, 2011 ; Ladha and Reddy, 1995 ; Rogers and Oldroyd, 2014 ). Although over last half a century, there have been ups and downs in the research efforts to assemble the N fixation mechanisms in cereals such as in rice and maize, ambitions have remained alive. Here, we briefly discuss various potential approaches and highlight most recent advances. Three major approaches, namely, (a) improving the endophytic bacteria that associate with plants to develop nodule-independent N2 -fixing systems, (b) developing root-nodule symbiosis in cereals, and (c) transferring the nitrogenase genes into cereal plants, are now being actively considered for realizing this target (Beatty and Good, 2011 ; Ladha and Reddy, 1995, 2003 ). Work on these three options has been discussed, and preliminary research was conducted in rice under the umbrella of global research project on Assessing Opportunities for Nitrogen Fixation in Rice during 1994–2001 (Ladha and Reddy, 2003 ). More recently, major breakthroughs in the genomics of diazotrophs and the genetics of nitrogen fixation, as well as the processes involved in legume-rhizobia symbioses, have opened new avenues to tackle this problem much more systematically. The first approach is to enhance the capabilities of root-colonizing bacteria to develop nitrogen-fixing microbes suitable for fertilizing the cereals. Recently, Setten et al. (2013)  engineered a root-colonizing non-diazotrophic endophyte, Pseudomonas protegens  Pf-5, by transferring a stretch of DNA with 52 genes, including a nif  gene cluster from Pseudomonas stutzeri  A1501, to develop a strain having the capability to fix the N constitutively, even in the presence of combined N, and able to release significant quantities of ammonium into the surrounding medium. Subsequently, in 2016, in greenhouse tests, Fox et al. (2017)  demonstrated increased yields in maize and wheat inoculated with this engineered strain and using 15 N isotope dilution analysis confirming that this beneficial effect is positively associated with N fixation in roots. The second approach considers the development of root-nodule symbiosis in non-legume crops such as rice and maize (Reddy et al., 2013 ; Rogers and Oldroyd, 2014 ). Researchers showed that rhizobia readily colonize the rhizosphere of rice, and can frequently enter in the roots to form endophytic associations (Reddy et al., 1997 ), and many of the elements that support nodular symbioses in legumes are also present in rice (Reddy et al., 2000a,b ; Sreevidya et al., 2006 ). Reddy et al. (1999)  showed that several legume ENOD  genes hybridized to DNA from a variety of rice genotypes, which was confirmed by isolating rice cDNAs showing considerable sequence homology to legume ENOD93  (Reddy et al., 1998 ) and ENOD40  (Kouchi et al., 1999 ). Interestingly, legumes with ability to establish N2  fixation symbiosis are also able to establish root endosymbioses with endomycorrhizal fungi (AMS), to acquire phosphorus. Recent studies showed that genetic elements required for two symbioses are common (Markmann et al., 2008 ; Reddy et al., 2013 ) suggesting that many of the components that require for rhizobial symbiosis may be conserved in rice and other plants. This common symbiotic signaling pathway provides an opportunity to investigate the possibilities for extending the mycorrhizal symbiotic genetic network of rice to accommodate rhizobial symbiosis for N fixation (Markmann et al., 2008 ; Reddy et al., 2013 ; Rogers and Oldroyd, 2014 ). The third approach for developing nitrogen-fixing crops considers the direct transfer of N-fixing (nif ) genes to synthesize and assemble functional nitrogenase in plants (Allen et al., 2017 ; Ivleva et al., 2016 ; López-Torrejón et al., 2016 ). Since N fixation is a high energy demanding process, and so chloroplasts (Merrick and Dixon, 1984 ) and mitochondria (Curatti and Rubio, 2014 ) are proposed to be the most appropriate sites for N fixation since they can meet the energy requirements for nitrogenase in plant cells. However, nitrogenase is extremely sensitive to oxygen and irreversibly inactivated in air, and so the oxygen evolved by chloroplasts during photosynthesis may be detrimental to the maintenance of the integrity of nitrogenase enzyme. Thus, expressing nitrogenase in chloroplasts may require temporal separation of N fixation and photosynthesis by restricting nif  gene expression to dark periods or, alternatively, by spatially restricting nif  gene expression to non-photosynthetic tissues such as root systems. Recently it has been shown that the eukaryotic electron-transport system (FNR–ferredoxin module) operating in plant chloroplasts and root plastids, rather than that operating in mitochondria, is able to support efficient functioning of nitrogenase, indicating suitability of plastids as a location for expressing this enzyme (Yang et al., 2017 ). On the other hand, strictly from the view of protection against oxygen, it has been suggested that localization/expression of nitrogenase in mitochondria, rather than in chloroplasts, may support efficient functioning of nitrogenase because of the near anoxic environment created in the mitochondrial matrix due to the respiratory activity of membranes (Curatti and Rubio, 2014 ). Not only a non-leguminous host plant is able to provide reducing power and energy needed to sustain nitrogenase catalysis, it should be able to demonstrate that a minimum set of genes can be transferred to a host to produce active nitrogenase components (Vicente and Dean, 2017 ). Undoubtedly, these recent advances in the field of nif  gene expression in eukaryotic systems allow us to be optimistic about achieving the goal of nitrogen-fixing cereal crops sooner than anticipated.  9.6 Enhancing NUE at field, farm and regional levels The need to optimize agricultural N use is fast emerging as a key global challenge. The overall goal of the new researches on N, therefore, should be to enhance NUE with a combination of agronomic and biological improvements aimed to raising NUE at field, farm and regional scales, and to demonstrate as to what extent these improvements will reduce the N losses to the environment, thereby contributing to the enhanced resilience of agriculture. Yet to achieve multi-scale improvements in NUE, a trans-disciplinary program linking complementary science areas and scales is required. On the genetic side, both the exploitation of natural variation and genetically modified (GM) crops offer opportunities to improve the plant NUE. On the agronomic side, a major thrust is the experimental demonstration of extent to which improving NUE simultaneously decreases different components of N losses. This is important, as different agronomic strategies will not affect all N pollution in the same way. Strategies may be identified, specifically aimed at reducing NH3  emissions (with additional benefits due to decreased particulate matter in the atmosphere and N deposition to nature), at reducing NO3 −  lost to groundwater (with a focus on better drinking water and less coastal pollution) or at reducing denitrification (reducing impacts on climate from N2 O emissions, while saving energy costs associated with N fertilizer manufacture by reducing N2  emissions). This integrated approach of optimizing the NUE will offer a “win-win-win” situation for the environment, food production and energy saving. Sutton et al. (2013)  developed the idea of “full chain nitrogen use efficiency” (NUEfc ), showing that a 20% increase in NUEfc  would lead to estimated global benefits worth US $170 billion per year, including $23 billion in N fertilizer-saving. They identified 10 “Key Actions” for improving NUE, including raising agronomic and biological NUE. Raising NUE will have major economic consequences. Till now the contributions of non-fertilizer sources have received less attention. Scientific efforts to improve agronomic NUE and biological NUE have too often been considered in isolation. However, genetic and biotechnological development should be considered in their wider agronomic context, and then consider the implications at field, farm and national scales. By taking such an integrative approach, the rationale should be to incorporate all relevant contributions to improving NUEfc . In this way, more N-efficient genetic stock and novel fertilizer practices will provide the foundation to show as to what can be done at the farm scale and for country as whole. This requires consideration of all potential N sources (including organic manures, BNF and atmospheric deposition), while exploring improved spatial and temporal strategies that can help optimize the N-resource use. The NUE should be addressed at a range of scales, from the plant, through the field, farm and national scales. This will allow us to assess as to what could be achieved by different NUE improvement strategies.   10 Conclusions In this paper, we established links between N fertilizer use in agricultural production systems and various SDGs set by the UNDP to end poverty, protect the Earth and ensure peace and prosperity. Nitrogen is perhaps the most essential nutrient in driving food production to meet human dietary needs. In the subsistence agriculture of the pre-industrial era, BNF and use of organic matter including manures were the primary sources of NR . But in today's more intensive agricultural systems, synthetic N has become a crucial addition to drive and sustain productivity. When applied in excess and without an ecosystem-based approach to N management, the NR  (ammonia, nitrate, nitrogen oxides) can cause significant ecological consequence as they are leaked to the environment through various processes causing multiple adverse effects on terrestrial and aquatic systems, in addition to human health. With the conflicting dual role of N—both positive and negative—the challenge is highly relevant across most of the SDGs. Globally, wide variation exists in the use of NR  ranging from insufficient use to maintain agroecosystem nutrient balances sustainably, such as in sub-Saharan Africa, to excess use as in China and India, among other countries. On the other hand, Europe and North America, with effective policies, have tended to more successfully reduce fertilizer N use without compromising crop productivity, although second generation environmental challenges still remain. We have dual challenge—(a) to encourage farming practices that improve soil health through the balanced use of organic matter, encouragement of supportive ecosystem services such as BNF, and intelligent use of fertilizer N in areas where widespread poverty, hunger and malnutrition warrant increasing crop production and economic growth, relating to SDGs 1, 2, 3 and 8. At the same time, there is a need to (b) optimize N use and minimize negative impacts in areas where crop productivity have peaked but also resulted in excess or misuse of N. Excess use of N can have severe consequences to public health, water bodies, energy use, cities, climate, and aquatic and terrestrial ecosystems, which are associated with SDGs 3, 6, 7, 11, 13, 14, and 15. Therefore, supporting SDG 12 (Responsible Production and Consumption) through sustainable N management is likely to have a positive feeding forward effect on other SDGs that are currently comprised by either too little or imbalanced N use. Moreover, progress in some of the goals related to education, equalities, infrastructure and investment, peace, and global partnerships. (e.g., SDGs 4, 5, 7, 9, 10, 16 and 17) can also create enabling conditions for more sustainable soils and N management. Recently, good methodological progress has been made in N budgeting and developing N use efficiency indices notably surplus N (sum of all N inputs minus N outputs or N yield). N budgeting in cereals has shown that globally cereals receive significant amounts of their N requirement from sources other than synthetic N which include non-symbiotic BNF, recycling through deposition, and crop residue/manure return. Each of these sources is biologically and climactically mediated and can be deliberately managed by farmers to improve N reserves in the soil and N supplies to the crop. Further research is needed to determine how these sources can be most effectively leveraged to support comprehensive N management strategies, including in the systems that are currently deficient in and can benefit from synthetic N application. The surplus N, which specifies N loss to the environment, is attractive because it is simple to calculate, readily understood, and based on easily available farm data. However, measurements of sources other than synthetic N pose challenge and the efficiency of use of other sources is yet to be determined. Globally, average NUE does not exceed 50%. Zhang et al. (2015)  estimated an NUE of 67% (an increase from 42%) to meet the global food demand in 2050 while keeping surplus N within the limits for maintaining acceptable air and water qualities to meet the SDGs. Achieving these targets of NUE look ambitious but appears to be technically feasible. There are also biological limits to achieve a very high NUE. Climatic and other agronomic conditions which are sub-optimal to crop production are just two of many complex reasons contributing to poor NUE. Most agricultural soils have considerably depleted levels of SOM. This is a central problem that results in agroecosystems losing their functionality and ability to retain and regulate the supply of N to crops. Poor knowledge and heavy price subsidies are also reasoning for the excess or misuse of N. Therefore, a combination of approaches of improved system management, right policies and good campaign/education are needed to improve NUE and to meet the SDGs. Nevertheless, there are successes as well as active efforts in pipeline to improve NUE and SOM management. Europe and North America are good examples where targeted policies and sustainable N management have reduced fertilizer N use but crop N harvest (or yields), and NUE have continued to improve. Recently, Several African Governments have brought agriculture back to the top of their development agenda and have reinstated input subsidy schemes aimed at promoting access to fertilizers, often alongside programs that emphasize organic matter and ecosystem-based management. China on the other hand has launched programs to improve soil fertility and the implementation of soil testing-based fertilization recommendations (STFR). In 2015, the Chinese Ministry of Agriculture introduced the campaign “Action to Achieve Zero Growth of Chemical Fertiliser Use by 2020.” Likewise, the GoI launched the Soil Health Card Scheme (https://soilhealth.dac.gov.in/ ) to encourage the farmers to apply the soil test-based fertilizers and soil amendments. More than 100 million soil cards were provided to the farmers carrying crop-wise recommendations of nutrients including N fertilizer to improve productivity through judicious use. Similarly, the GoI has mandated all urea (primary source of N fertilizer in India) to be neem-coated for slow N release and improve NUE. Combined with advances in digital soil mapping and new tools to enable rapid diagnostics of nutrient variability at large spatial scales, increasingly appropriate recommendations can be generated to assist farmers in making balanced use of nutrients. Undoubtedly, much progress has been made in developing technologies for an efficient N management, which along with good agronomy are proven to enhance crop N harvest and NUE with lower surplus N. While there are numerous technologies that have been developed and may be appropriate for use by farmers, delivery mechanisms and methods to encourage spontaneous adaptation and adoption need to be strengthened. An equally or perhaps more importantly, approach is needed to create awareness and educate senior officials, policy makers, extension personnel, and farmers on the impact of appropriate soil management and intelligent use of N fertilizer, in conjunction with biologically integrated strategies for soil fertility maintenance. An effective and aggressive campaign against misuse of N will be effective where excess N is used. On the other hand, improved accessibility of N fertilizer and policies to move farmers toward soil quality improvement in regions where N use is sub-optimal are urgently needed. Additionally, further research is needed to quantify strength of linkages between sustainable N management and the system SDGs, going beyond identification of connections between individual goals and NR  flows.
